  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6e4e3025-fc9a-493c-8e95-da57218d3351'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer13452173645429238323">Beneath the majestic towers of the Alhambra, Granada's cobbled streets resonate with music and secrets. Sonia Cameron knows nothing of the city's shocking past; she is here to dance.   <span id="freeText13452173645429238323" style="display:none">Beneath the majestic towers of the Alhambra, Granada's cobbled streets resonate with music and secrets. Sonia Cameron knows nothing of the city's shocking past; she is here to dance. But in a quiet café, a chance conversation and an intriguing collection of old photographs draw her into the extraordinary tale of Spain's devastating civil war.<br /><br />Seventy years earlier, the café is home to the close-knit Ramírez family. In 1936, an army coup led by Franco shatters the country's fragile peace, and in the heart of Granada the family witnesses the worst atrocities of conflict. Divided by politics and tragedy, everyone must take a side, fighting a personal battle as Spain rips itself apart.<br /><br />Captivating and deeply moving, Victoria Hislop's second novel is as inspiring as her international bestselling debut, <i>The Island</i>.</span>

<div id="review_67106539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/67106539" />
    <a title="Tara Chevrestt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2544368-tara-chevrestt"><img alt="Tara Chevrestt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399003936p2/2544368.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/67106539?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara Chevrestt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara Chevrestt" href="/user/show/2544368-tara-chevrestt">Tara Chevrestt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2544368-tara-chevrestt?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2544368-tara-chevrestt?shelf=blew-me-away-list">blew-me-away-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2544368-tara-chevrestt?shelf=spain">spain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer67106539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12989464574708399884">After being blown away by "The Island" I immediately ordered "The Return" and I am not disappointed. I am now an official Victoria Hislop fan. Her novels are to be savored and enjoyed. <br/><br/>"The Return" begins in recent times in England. Readers are introduced to a middle aged woman named Sonia. This part is very similiar to "The Island" as both heroines are having relationship issues and are both facing similiar difficult choices.. basically "stay with this jerk or leave" type choices. I found parts</span>
  <span id="freeText12989464574708399884" style="display:none">After being blown away by "The Island" I immediately ordered "The Return" and I am not disappointed. I am now an official Victoria Hislop fan. Her novels are to be savored and enjoyed. <br /><br />"The Return" begins in recent times in England. Readers are introduced to a middle aged woman named Sonia. This part is very similiar to "The Island" as both heroines are having relationship issues and are both facing similiar difficult choices.. basically "stay with this jerk or leave" type choices. I found parts of the book regarding Sonia rather predictable but that did not deter me. Sonia and her friend take up salsa dancing and after finding some old pictures of her mother and taking a trip to Spain, Sonia becomes fascinated with the story of the Ramirez family. <br /><br />The middle part of the book is about the Ramirez family and Spain's Civil War in the 1930s. There is a mother, Concha and father, Pablo. They run a cafe and for a while things are wonderful in their life. Their oldest son, Antonio is a teacher. Their middle son, Ignacio is a bull fighter. Their youngest son, Emilio is slowly taking over the cafe and has a passion for music. The daughter, Mercedes is a talented flamenco dancer. The Ramirez family's world shatters with the beginning of the war as their sons oppose each other, betray each other, and one by one, the family members are arrested, killed, or face some life changing complication due to the war. <br /><br />There is a romance between Mercedes and a guitarist, Javier. She spends the duration of the war searching for her love and taking many risks to find him. She never loses her love of dancing tho and she brightens many a person's day with her skills. Even in times of war, one must find joy and express it. <br /><br />The last part of the novel takes readers back to present day England and Sonia must make a difficult choice after making some surprising revelations. Will the story of the Ramirez family inspire her somehow? <br /><br />A beautiful novel and a fabulous look at the life of Spain, the passion behind flamenco, the risks behind bullfighting, and the trials families face in civil war. <br /><br />Just like an appreciative audience watching a flamenco dancer tap and twirl or a bullfighter swing his cape, I say "Ole! Ole!" to this fine novel. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12989464574708399884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_353320637" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/353320637" />
    <a title="Veronica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2153853-veronica"><img alt="Veronica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245687842p2/2153853.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/353320637?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veronica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veronica" href="/user/show/2153853-veronica">Veronica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2153853-veronica?shelf=bookcrossing">bookcrossing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2153853-veronica?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer353320637" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4720638019168277153">Oh dear. This just doesn't work at all. The modern-day story wrapped around the historical part is so flimsy and cliched that it would have been better to leave it out altogether and just tell the civil war story. A basic structural flaw was that Miguel simply could not have known all the details given here. Every now and then,Hislop remembers, oops, Miguel is supposed to be telling the story here, so she flips back to the present day for a paragraph so that they can order another coffee. Could </span>
  <span id="freeText4720638019168277153" style="display:none">Oh dear. This just doesn't work at all. The modern-day story wrapped around the historical part is so flimsy and cliched that it would have been better to leave it out altogether and just tell the civil war story. A basic structural flaw was that Miguel simply could not have known all the details given here. Every now and then,Hislop remembers, oops, Miguel is supposed to be telling the story here, so she flips back to the present day for a paragraph so that they can order another coffee. Could he really have told this whole story in such detail over a period of a few hours? It just doesn't ring true.<br /><br />But if you're going to do that, at least make it a genuine story, not a potted history. Hislop had done her research, but then just regurgitated it onto the page. Large parts of it were just like reading a history book. The characters never left the page to become real people with hopes and desires. It was impossible to feel moved by deaths and imprisonment.<br /><br />And finally, I don't have a problem with coincidences in novels, or indeed in real life (hey, I enjoy <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10015.Kate_Atkinson" title="Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Kate Atkinson</a>, queen of coincidences!). But the coincidences on which this novel relies are just too contrived, and you can see the "twist" coming a mile off. I ended up flipping quickly through the last coupel of hundred pages to have my suspicions confirmed.<br /><br />But I'm giving it two stars instead of one, because:<br />1. The descriptions of flamenco are so well done. It's obvious that Hislop is passionate about this dance, and the novel only really comes alive in these passages.<br />2. A lot of people who like this book say they didn't know anything about the Spanish Civil War. If the popularity of Hislop's work means that more people know about it, that's a good thing. Her heart is in the right place, she did her homework, she just doesn't have the novelistic skill to carry it off.<br /><br />However, if you want to read a brilliant novel that conveys the true horror and tragedy of the civil war, and its effect on families, don't read this. Read Almudena Grandes' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9682371.The_Frozen_Heart" title="The Frozen Heart by Almudena Grandes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Frozen Heart</a> instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4720638019168277153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_73779727" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73779727" />
    <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield"><img alt="Lance Greenfield" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442658682p2/2793431.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73779727?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lance Greenfield" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield">Lance Greenfield</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2793431-lance-greenfield?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73779727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3947184757312471824">Gripping account of the Spanish Civil War<br/><br/>The body of this book consists the story of the Ramirez family of Granada during the Spanish Republic, the Civil War and the ensuing years. It is a tremendously powerful narrative and cannot fail to stir the emotions. The detail reflects the huge effort that the author must have put into her research.<br/><br/>The vehicle for the main story, which is the journey of Sonia, as she tries to fill the gap left in her life by her empty marriage with an adventure into fla</span>
  <span id="freeText3947184757312471824" style="display:none">Gripping account of the Spanish Civil War<br /><br />The body of this book consists the story of the Ramirez family of Granada during the Spanish Republic, the Civil War and the ensuing years. It is a tremendously powerful narrative and cannot fail to stir the emotions. The detail reflects the huge effort that the author must have put into her research.<br /><br />The vehicle for the main story, which is the journey of Sonia, as she tries to fill the gap left in her life by her empty marriage with an adventure into flamenco dancing in Andalucía in the company of her old school friend, is just too improbable to be believable. It is even more incredible that the reader is expected to believe that Sonia hears the whole story of the Ramirez family from the waiter of a back-street Grenadine café in a SINGLE day. As if this weren't enough of an integrity challenge, we are further asked to believe the strong connections between the Ramirez family and Sonia, which are inevitably revealed towards the end.<br /><br />Despite all of this, The Return, is just such a wonderfully moving story, and every single atom of the trials and tribulations of the Ramirez family is totally believable. In fact, it was so believable that I could almost feel the pain and passion that I imagined the members of that family must have felt. I have always been a big fan of historical fiction as it really brings the past to life in a way that dry school books could never do. This story, with all of its sadness and joy, is just SO moving.<br /><br />I strongly recommend this book to everyone I know, as I am sure that they will not be disappointed. To those of you reading this review who are unknown to me, I say, PLEASE read it! If my review sent you the wrong way, just add a comment to that effect. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3947184757312471824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87718271?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780755332953" href="/book/show/6327716-the-return">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=spain">spain</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=disliked">disliked</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87718271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6608052964923270146">I DIDN'T like this, not at all!!! Look, I gave it a very good fair chance...... I can think of two positive things to say about this book. <br/>1. Flamenco is superbly described with an emtion that totally pulls the reader in.<br/>2. The reader is given a clear resumé of the time leading up Spanish Civil War 1936-1939, the Civil War itself and a teeny, teeny bit about its aftermath. BUT, it reads like a school book for children. Simplistic. The story is simply a showcase for the events of the war. After r</span>
  <span id="freeText6608052964923270146" style="display:none">I DIDN'T like this, not at all!!! Look, I gave it a very good fair chance...... I can think of two positive things to say about this book. <br />1. Flamenco is superbly described with an emtion that totally pulls the reader in.<br />2. The reader is given a clear resumé of the time leading up Spanish Civil War 1936-1939, the Civil War itself and a teeny, teeny bit about its aftermath. BUT, it reads like a school book for children. Simplistic. The story is simply a showcase for the events of the war. After reading this I wonder how CAN the Catholic belief remain so prominent in Spain today?! Perhaps <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823066.Ghosts_of_Spain_Travels_Through_a_Country_s_Hidden_Past" title="Ghosts of Spain Travels Through a Country's Hidden Past by Giles Tremlett" rel="nofollow noopener">Ghosts of Spain: Travels Through a Country's Hidden Past</a> will answer my question and give an indepth analysis of how Spaniards have dealt with their war experiences. Maybe their religious convictions have altered.<br /><br />OK, here is what I absolutely didn't like about this book. From page 1 to page 578, the story is told, not experienced (except for the bits about flamenco)! I never cared for any of the main characters, except maybe a teeny bit for Mercedes' mother. I absolutely did not feel for any of them. As I said, there are two thread, a modern and an historical thread. The modern one is shallow and contrived. None of the characters display any depth. The writing style is O-R-D-I-N-A-R-Y, as blah as you can get! Corrida(bullfighting) is described. Yes here I was moved, but it was so utterly HORRIBLE. The depiction was nauseating. I must say, how this could have been so loved in Spain, makes me wonder about such a culture......<br /><br />I am going to give the Spanish Civil War theme one more try. I will read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7893692.Stone_in_a_Landslide" title="Stone in a Landslide by Maria Barbal" rel="nofollow noopener">Stone in a Landslide</a> next. Maybe I will not be satisfied until I have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/823066.Ghosts_of_Spain_Travels_Through_a_Country_s_Hidden_Past" title="Ghosts of Spain Travels Through a Country's Hidden Past by Giles Tremlett" rel="nofollow noopener">Ghosts of Spain: Travels Through a Country's Hidden Past</a>, but I have told myself to stop buying books ......<br /><br />I have only read 52 pages. The story itself feels rather mundane and just typical fiction. There are two strands to the story, a modern on e and one concerning the Spanish Civil War. So far the story only follows the modern tale, BUT there is one thing that is amazing. I have never seen flamenco dancing, and yet this text makes me believe that I hear it, see it, smell the sweat in the room and feel the tension. The passion, anger and grief inherent to this dance are expertly depicted by the author. The dancing sucks you in. So far, that is all that sucks me in...... </span>
  <a data-text-id="6608052964923270146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Meira Eliot" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3719467-meira-eliot"><img alt="Meira Eliot" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394144889p2/3719467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/102591119?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102591119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10015083259344006928">It's almost as if countries can have karma, like people do. While the rest of us Europeans were still in the Dark Ages, Spain was a multi-ethnic island of tolerance and excellence, safeguarding the legacy of the Greeks through Arab translations. Then along come Ferdinand and Isabella, chuck out the Jews and the Muslims in the same year as Columbus discovered America, and the upshot is a quagmire of bigotry and intolerance that is still going on in the 20th century in the form of the Spanish Civi</span>
  <span id="freeText10015083259344006928" style="display:none">It's almost as if countries can have karma, like people do. While the rest of us Europeans were still in the Dark Ages, Spain was a multi-ethnic island of tolerance and excellence, safeguarding the legacy of the Greeks through Arab translations. Then along come Ferdinand and Isabella, chuck out the Jews and the Muslims in the same year as Columbus discovered America, and the upshot is a quagmire of bigotry and intolerance that is still going on in the 20th century in the form of the Spanish Civil War. The story is told as the history of a single family in retrospect, which gives perspective and poignance to the horrors described as the war unfolds. Spain is a country that gets into the blood on the first visit - not the bars and beaches, but the majesty of the landscapes and the Mediterranean pace of life. This novel shows us that Europe still has a lot of unresolved issues to do with its faiths and politics. I fear that fascism is not yet dead. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10015083259344006928" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_260675508" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/260675508" />
    <a title="Meenoo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6982645-meenoo"><img alt="Meenoo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325532008p2/6982645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/260675508?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meenoo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meenoo" href="/user/show/6982645-meenoo">Meenoo</a>
      </span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer260675508" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10181456462454130457">There are many things to like about The Return, but also some things that were too predictable and required a willing suspension of belief. The good parts: the descriptions of flamenco were wonderful. I know so little about this dance and I learned a lot. I could tell that she definitely did her research about the Spanish Civil War, which is also an era of history I know too little about. All of my knowledge about this era comes from "The Shadow of the Wind" and the movie "Pan's Labyrinth." Howe</span>
  <span id="freeText10181456462454130457" style="display:none">There are many things to like about The Return, but also some things that were too predictable and required a willing suspension of belief. The good parts: the descriptions of flamenco were wonderful. I know so little about this dance and I learned a lot. I could tell that she definitely did her research about the Spanish Civil War, which is also an era of history I know too little about. All of my knowledge about this era comes from "The Shadow of the Wind" and the movie "Pan's Labyrinth." However, because I suspect much of her audience does not know about Spanish history, much of the book felt like a history textbook, especially the parts involving Antonio. I found myself skimming those parts because I felt that it was written from a hindsight perspective.<br />The ending was neat and exactly what should have happened (and exactly what I expected). Still, I loved reading about Granada and flamenco, and I hope I get to see it in person (although preferably without all that fascism).</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mummy Loves Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mummy Loves Books" href="/user/show/10781301-mummy-loves-books">Mummy Loves Books</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer640381103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14199647821609595415">I really enjoyed Victoria Hislops The Island so as soon as I saw this had been released I put my name down at the library to borrow a copy. The book started well as we follow Sonia, an unhappily married woman, to Granada where she and a friend have booked a holiday and some dance lessons. We follow her as she becomes more immersed in the salsa and flamenco dances and as she meets an elderly cafe owner who begins to tell her about the family who owned the cafe at the time of the civil war.<br/><br/>At this</span>
  <span id="freeText14199647821609595415" style="display:none">I really enjoyed Victoria Hislops The Island so as soon as I saw this had been released I put my name down at the library to borrow a copy. The book started well as we follow Sonia, an unhappily married woman, to Granada where she and a friend have booked a holiday and some dance lessons. We follow her as she becomes more immersed in the salsa and flamenco dances and as she meets an elderly cafe owner who begins to tell her about the family who owned the cafe at the time of the civil war.<br /><br />At this point to be honest I began to get a bit confused, having no previous visits to Spain or knowledge of the Spanish Civil War to work from I war relying on the author to indicate clearly what happened, but I got a bit lost between who was on which side and what they stood for and as a result found myself scanning some of the pages whilst not fulling taking in the setting. I fully enjoyed her characterisations and this kept me reading when I otherwise may have given up.<br /><br />The story of Mercedes the young Spanish girl whos love of dance and a young guitarist named Javier takes her into danger during a war which has destroyed her family is excellent but I finished the book feeling I'd missed some of the crucial elements because Hislop's explanation of the history of the war wasn't gripping enough to keep me reading every single word.<br /><br />I also felt that the end of the book was a little rushed, with Sonia's story being a little pushed into the last chapter and a bit. Considering the time invested in introducing this character at the start of the book it didn't do her justice to have it crammed into this short space.<br /><br />I would read another Victoria Hislop novel but this certainly is not The Island<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14199647821609595415" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Annemieke Windt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4923399-annemieke-windt"><img alt="Annemieke Windt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325012377p2/4923399.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/296571248?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annemieke Windt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annemieke Windt" href="/user/show/4923399-annemieke-windt">Annemieke Windt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer296571248" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14255365988117251397">I struggle to come up with an opinion about Victoria Hislop's The Return. It took me weeks to finish it, which isn't a good omen because she knows to write with a certain pace. The storyline is intriguing, because it deals with a partly forgotten war in the rest of Europe, the Spanish cival war. That by itself should offer enough drama, but the main part of the book, telling the story of the Ramirez family from Granada, feels different. As a reader I felt that I witnessed everything through a wi</span>
  <span id="freeText14255365988117251397" style="display:none">I struggle to come up with an opinion about Victoria Hislop's The Return. It took me weeks to finish it, which isn't a good omen because she knows to write with a certain pace. The storyline is intriguing, because it deals with a partly forgotten war in the rest of Europe, the Spanish cival war. That by itself should offer enough drama, but the main part of the book, telling the story of the Ramirez family from Granada, feels different. As a reader I felt that I witnessed everything through a window clouded over with condense. And the frame she used: letting someone from the present stumble across the story from the past doesn't work, because the mixture of present and past doesn't work well. To me it seemed like a formula that didn't really work.<br /><br />Which is a pity, because a couple of years ago I read The Island and was moved by it and loved the story. But although the backdrop is different, Spain instead of Greece, it that same strain of the story. With one big difference this novel just doesn't work that well for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14255365988117251397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1701998706" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13455085929359376785">This is an ambitious book covering much of the history of the gruesome Spanish civil war. It opens in the present with a more light-hearted subject: dancing. Thirty-five-year-old Londoner Sonia and her wild-child schoolmate Maggie have taken up salsa. They head to Granada to attend a course, much to the disapproval of Sonia's husband James, who reckons she should concentrate her energies on hosting dinner parties and providing a son and heir. While Maggie throws herself into the holiday pleasure</span>
  <span id="freeText13455085929359376785" style="display:none">This is an ambitious book covering much of the history of the gruesome Spanish civil war. It opens in the present with a more light-hearted subject: dancing. Thirty-five-year-old Londoner Sonia and her wild-child schoolmate Maggie have taken up salsa. They head to Granada to attend a course, much to the disapproval of Sonia's husband James, who reckons she should concentrate her energies on hosting dinner parties and providing a son and heir. While Maggie throws herself into the holiday pleasures of drink, dance and dalliance, Sonia is beguiled by the city's brooding sense of past secrets and by its own dancing tradition. As the story unfolds we realise that Sonia has her own connection with the past and that it is calling to her restless and dissatisfied soul. I enjoyed reading this as I knew practically nothing about the Spanish Civil war and the story was heart warming and affectionate.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15758634627751994480">The Return feels as if it’s almost told in two parts. In the present day you meet Sonia. She’s a middle-aged woman who is having relationship issues and is facing some tough decisions when it comes to her marriage. She is visiting Spain with a friend and they decide to take some dancing classes to celebrate her friend’s birthday.<br/><br/>While on her trip she meets an elderly waiter at a cafe who tells her the story of the Ramirez family and the Spanish Civil War in the 1930s. Pablo and Concha Ramirez ru</span>
  <span id="freeText15758634627751994480" style="display:none">The Return feels as if it’s almost told in two parts. In the present day you meet Sonia. She’s a middle-aged woman who is having relationship issues and is facing some tough decisions when it comes to her marriage. She is visiting Spain with a friend and they decide to take some dancing classes to celebrate her friend’s birthday.<br /><br />While on her trip she meets an elderly waiter at a cafe who tells her the story of the Ramirez family and the Spanish Civil War in the 1930s. Pablo and Concha Ramirez run a cafe and lead a happy life with their children - Antonio who is a teacher, Ignacio the bull fighter, Emilio who is in line to take over the cafe and, their daughter, Mercedes, who is a talented flamenco dancer.<br /><br />This second part of the book tells the story of the Ramirez's and how their world and lives are affected by the war. Plenty of family drama ensues - disagreements, betrayals and eventually deaths.<br /><br />Ms. Hislop’s writing is so vividly detailed that you feel as if you are whisked away on an epic adventure to Spain where you have a front seat to bull fights, flamenco dancing and a stroll through the streets of Granada. My favorite character was Mercedes - her love for Flamenco and her guitarist Javier were an inspiration. I really didn't know much about the Spanish Civil War and this book had a lot of insight into that time - I actually felt I learned something while reading it.<br /><br />I highly enjoyed this and recommend it to anyone - there's a little bit of everything (love, hate, drama, violence, adventure) in it, so it can definitely appease a wide variety of readers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15758634627751994480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer2880738533765373316">This was a painful read; got a lot more than I'd asked for. <br/>The book's strength is completely in the flashback telling of Franco's rise in pre-WWII Spain. I was completely ignorant of his torturous reign and it got-to-me in it's intensely graphic and devastating familial context. Never-the-less, I highly recommend it. <br/>The dovetailing story of flamenco over two generations, and the modern British romances (wait, is this last an oxymoron?) work well, but Hislop has a clunky hand in the love arena </span>
  <span id="freeText2880738533765373316" style="display:none">This was a painful read; got a lot more than I'd asked for. <br />The book's strength is completely in the flashback telling of Franco's rise in pre-WWII Spain. I was completely ignorant of his torturous reign and it got-to-me in it's intensely graphic and devastating familial context. Never-the-less, I highly recommend it. <br />The dovetailing story of flamenco over two generations, and the modern British romances (wait, is this last an oxymoron?) work well, but Hislop has a clunky hand in the love arena and her awkwardness at times seems laughable - good thing, since her historical recounting is ruthless and needs a modern break in action. The shifts from present to flashback are artless and don't segue as well as I would've edited them ... which makes me wonder why they passed into publication.<br />Author rec'd letters from people who recalled and confirmed the brutal details of that era, and these add grace to the end of the story. <br />NOT light beach reading, but excellent. All high schools should require it in European history syllabus. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2880738533765373316" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9747760814598729825">There are lots of things to like about this book, and yet I can only give it three stars because it just didn't quite grip me. "The Return" is not one of those books that you "just can't put down" --- I actually had to make myself pick it up and keep reading most of the time.<br/><br/>And yet --- this is not a bad book. The style of writing is good, and the characters are interesting. A few years ago I spent 2 weeks in Granada, so I could really see the streets and Plazas of the city - as well as the magn</span>
  <span id="freeText9747760814598729825" style="display:none">There are lots of things to like about this book, and yet I can only give it three stars because it just didn't quite grip me. "The Return" is not one of those books that you "just can't put down" --- I actually had to make myself pick it up and keep reading most of the time.<br /><br />And yet --- this is not a bad book. The style of writing is good, and the characters are interesting. A few years ago I spent 2 weeks in Granada, so I could really see the streets and Plazas of the city - as well as the magnificent Alhambra - in my mind.<br /><br />As a reader you learn a lot about Spain in the 1930s --- something I really didn't know anything about before I started reading this book.<br /><br />All in all, it's a good enough book, but one that I will probably soon have forgotten (something that doesn't happen with really great books), and that will definitely not be a keeper on my book shelf.<br /><br />I guess "The Return" just misses that "special something" that makes a book great and memorable ...</span>

<div id="review_1906712105" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1906712105" />
    <a title="Sheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6970638-sheri"><img alt="Sheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439557718p2/6970638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1906712105?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sheri" href="/user/show/6970638-sheri">Sheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6970638-sheri?shelf=poverty-class-issues">poverty-class-issues</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1906712105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15662106573684441030">So this is really two books; a modern framework as a vehicle for telling a story about the Spanish civil war. The historical fiction part is really quite good; I would rate the middle section of the book a 4 star. The characters are real and the history is nice. I knew next to nothing about the Spanish civil war and besides getting a good history lesson, I liked the story. The modern framework, however is a 2 star. The characters are shells and the story is silly. Who cares about Sonia and James</span>
  <span id="freeText15662106573684441030" style="display:none">So this is really two books; a modern framework as a vehicle for telling a story about the Spanish civil war. The historical fiction part is really quite good; I would rate the middle section of the book a 4 star. The characters are real and the history is nice. I knew next to nothing about the Spanish civil war and besides getting a good history lesson, I liked the story. The modern framework, however is a 2 star. The characters are shells and the story is silly. Who cares about Sonia and James' marriage problems, they are not really anything other than stereotypes. And the whole ridiculousness of Mercedes turning out to be Sonia's mother was hogwash. I don't understand why Hislop didn't just write the Ramirez family story; the fact that she needed to ensconce it in a modern envelope and then make that modern envelope so shallow diminished the rest of it. I average at a 3 star rating, but was almost annoyed enough to make it a 2 star.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15662106573684441030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14641812308884980715">Sonia married to an older man, James, is bored with her routine married life. Trapped in a loveless marriage, she finds an escape in the weekly salsa class. She enjoys dancing and looks forward to the class much to chagrin of her husband. She levels up and is soon joined by her best friend, Maggie.<br/><br/>Maggie has a brilliant idea. To celebrate her upcoming 35th birthday in Spain and joining a local dance class.<br/><br/>Maggie and Sonia both go to Granada, while Maggie is exhilarated with the dancing and Spani</span>
  <span id="freeText14641812308884980715" style="display:none">Sonia married to an older man, James, is bored with her routine married life. Trapped in a loveless marriage, she finds an escape in the weekly salsa class. She enjoys dancing and looks forward to the class much to chagrin of her husband. She levels up and is soon joined by her best friend, Maggie.<br /><br />Maggie has a brilliant idea. To celebrate her upcoming 35th birthday in Spain and joining a local dance class.<br /><br />Maggie and Sonia both go to Granada, while Maggie is exhilarated with the dancing and Spanish men, Sonia is contemplating her marriage. During one of her walks to see the city, she meets Miguel, owner of a cafe. He introduces her to Spanish history of Civil War and three siblings, Anthony, Emilio and Mercedes.<br /><br />She "returns" to know more about Mercedes, who she thinks is her mother. A mother, whose past she knows nothing about.<br /><br />Victoria Hislop handles the heavy civil war story and the love of dancing very well. The story of the war makes you shudder and the dance enchants you.</span>

<div id="review_2982197430" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2982197430" />
    <a title="gia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62752166-gia"><img alt="gia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567795316p2/62752166.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2982197430?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="gia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="gia" href="/user/show/62752166-gia">gia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780755332953" href="/book/show/6327716-the-return">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2982197430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13742452839181884111">Hislop just writes about history with such authenticity, such authority and at times, such sadness that you can’t help but be brought in. She brings everything that I loved about The Island, her first novel, and transplanted it into a devastating story set during Spain’s Civil War. <br/><br/>A family saga that is about a family slowly being ripped apart is compulsively readable, despite its subject matter. What makes the whole thing work is that although the reader gets a broad overview of the conflict, t</span>
  <span id="freeText13742452839181884111" style="display:none">Hislop just writes about history with such authenticity, such authority and at times, such sadness that you can’t help but be brought in. She brings everything that I loved about The Island, her first novel, and transplanted it into a devastating story set during Spain’s Civil War. <br /><br />A family saga that is about a family slowly being ripped apart is compulsively readable, despite its subject matter. What makes the whole thing work is that although the reader gets a broad overview of the conflict, the focus remains on the family throughout. Seeing the effects of the war and the divisions it wrought among families is ultimately devastating and what makes this such a brilliant and heart wrenching read. For me anyway, it slightly edges over The Island, which is not altogether a bad thing. I couldn’t help but love it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13742452839181884111" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16821272551094093952">It was an interesting read as I knew next to nothing about the Spanish Civil War. However, I did feel that the book was overlong and I skipped quite a few pages. Still feel her best was The Island, which was brilliant.</span>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2282759636" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2282759636" />
    <a title="Siddhant Nath" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39336200-siddhant-nath"><img alt="Siddhant Nath" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493705033p2/39336200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2282759636?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siddhant Nath" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siddhant Nath" href="/user/show/39336200-siddhant-nath">Siddhant Nath</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2282759636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10536759836064586016">There's a certain trepidation that accompanies the cynical man as he reads an account of love. He wants to enjoy it but every fiber of his being screams of such a tale being impossible and far fetched. No matter how real the premise or how stark the depiction, reading love stories is never easy for him. He feels disconnected and even bored, the quality of the story notwithstanding. Every love story, thus, is viewed by him as cliche ridden and familiar, like reading a newspaper article whose head</span>
  <span id="freeText10536759836064586016" style="display:none">There's a certain trepidation that accompanies the cynical man as he reads an account of love. He wants to enjoy it but every fiber of his being screams of such a tale being impossible and far fetched. No matter how real the premise or how stark the depiction, reading love stories is never easy for him. He feels disconnected and even bored, the quality of the story notwithstanding. Every love story, thus, is viewed by him as cliche ridden and familiar, like reading a newspaper article whose headline is enough to gauge what has happened.<br /><br />Victoria Hislop's The Return evoked no such emotions from this cynic. Well, almost, but that's good enough as well. The Return is moving in its description of human emotion, stark in relating to us the ravages of war and ultimately successful in telling a story of love through one of the most violent times in world history. There are hiccups, some bigger than most, but they are thankfully few enough for even a cynic like myself to ignore and let the impact of this tale wash over.<br /><br />Weak points first, which can probably be summarised by just one word - Sonia. An English woman travels to Spain to learn dancing, something that has provided her an escape from her monotonous and borderline abusive relationship with her husband. In Spain she finds a man by the name of Miguel who decides to tell her a story which forms the major part of the book. While the story Miguel tells us is worth investing in, Sonia's transition from a person 'just living' to the ultimate 'I must escape' position seems rushed and not deserving. We aren't given enough time with her and as a result, the character doesn't feel fleshed out despite having crystal clear motivations. An argument could be made that the character's sudden shift acts as a mirror to the reader; I mean I felt like taking up dancing myself after I finished reading. But...cynic, remember?<br /><br />Apart from that, the story flowers with depth and a thoroughly (and surprising) realistic depiction of a country torn apart by Civil War. To put it in simple words, this is a story about the Spanish Civil War told through the eyes and sorrows of one Spanish family, who were politically aligned with those who 'lost' the Civil War. There are pages about people migrating to other cities on foot after their houses are destroyed, friends dying alongside friends, mothers watching their families brutalised or killed and amongst all of this, is a story of love.<br /><br />The love story of a man and woman are part of this story's heart but there is so much more than that. Love for tradition, friends, family, country, belief and peace are some of the many themes that resonate throughout this book. Full credit to Hislop for juggling all of these and never dropping them once. The story of the Ramirez family will hit hard, move you, drive you to tears and make you smile widely. Such is the range and scope of this story.<br /><br />The Return has a love story within its heart but it's more of a story about the power of love. While the present story line of the book isn't as compelling, the Ramirez family's saga makes up for that in leaps and bounds. What you get finally, is a story that stays with you long after you've closed the book and gone to sleep. A must-read for anyone, especially fans of historical fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10536759836064586016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Lf1OnFcI/fzSbOpVNgEFL4UpFqEZsWq0klU+v8ECJSXQ9fPNpnJ5njkKPKrPi1cKOOujKTLRn1M6N5uekvYHxg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Men under klærne skjuler hun et grotesk fødselsmerke. Det er som om hun har sittet i djevelens hånd. Med håp om å bli som alle andre flykter Jamilet til USA. Hun får som jobb å ta seg av den gamle Señor Peregrino. Når han forteller om sin pilegrimsvandring til Santiago de Compostella, forandres alt. \n\nSeñor Peregrino er en poetisk og overraskende fortelling om aldri å miste troen på kjærligheten. Om å våge å leve på den skjøre linjen mellom fantasi og virkelighet. Historien om Jamilet fortsetter i La Peregrina. \n\nOm forfatteren\nCecilia Samartin (født 1961) vokste opp på Cuba midt under revolusjonen, før hun flyktet til USA. Hun er utdannet familieterapeut, og har jobbet med et prosjekt som skal bedre levevilkårene for de aller fattigste i Los Angeles. Hun debuterte som forfatter i 2004 med romanen Drømmehjerte.\ne birthmark. It spills over her back and down her legs, twisting and writhing like a hideous cape of blood, causing her to be shunned by the villagers of her rural Mexican town. In search of medical salvation, this angel with the devil\'s mark is finally driven to escape north and cross the border illegally to Los Angeles.After acquiring false documents, Jamilet finds work at a mental hospital, where she is assigned to look after Señor Peregrino, an elderly man from Spain who is as disagreeable as he is mysterious. Jamilet is given strict orders to keep her distance, but when he cleverly snags possession of her papers, he bargains to return them upon the condition that she listen to his story. Jamilet begrudgingly agrees, and Señor Peregrino takes her back to the days of his youth, when he embarked upon a mystical and romantic journey along the legendary Road to Santiago in Spain. Jamilet and Señor Peregrino forge a spiritual bond that is more healing to them both than modern medicine could ever be. In an inspiring story of redemption, faith and the enduring power of love, Samartin offers an enlightening perspective on the true meaning of beauty.<\/span>\n  <a Travelling from lonely Ladakh, high up in the Himalayas, Nerys discovers a new world in the lakeside city of Srinagar. Here, in the exquisite heart of Kashmir, the British live on carved wooden houseboats and dance, flirt and gossip as if there is no war. But the battles draw ever closer, and life in Srinagar becomes less frivolous when the men are sent away to fight. Nerys is caught up in a dangerous friendship, and by the time she is reunited with her husband, the innocent Welsh bride has become a different woman. Years later, when Mair Ellis clears out her father\'s house, she finds an exquisite antique shawl, woven from the finest yarns and embroidered in the shades of lake water and mountain skies. Wrapped within its folds is a lock of child\'s hair. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18177494321155605487">Victoria Hislop read English at Oxford, and worked in publishing, PR and as a journalist before becoming a novelist. She is married with two children.<br/><br/>Her first novel, The Island, held the number one slot in the Sunday Times paperback charts for eight consecutive weeks and has sold over two million copies worldwide. Victoria was the Newcomer of the Year at the Galaxy British Book Awards 2007 and wo</span>
  <span id="freeText18177494321155605487" style="display:none">Victoria Hislop read English at Oxford, and worked in publishing, PR and as a journalist before becoming a novelist. She is married with two children.<br /><br />Her first novel, The Island, held the number one slot in the Sunday Times paperback charts for eight consecutive weeks and has sold over two million copies worldwide. Victoria was the Newcomer of the Year at the Galaxy British Book Awards 2007 and won the Richard &amp; Judy Summer Read competition.<br /><br />Her second novel, The Return, was also a Sunday Times number one bestseller, and her books have been translated into more than twenty languages. A short story collection, One Cretan Evening, was published in September and both a third novel, The Thread is published in English in October and in Greek in November 2011.</span>
